<p><b>Italy</b> (<a href="/wiki/Italian_language" title="Italian language">Italian</a>: <span lang="it" xml:lang="it"><i>Italia</i></span>), officially the <b>Italian Republic</b>; (<a href="/wiki/Italian_language" title="Italian language">Italian</a>: <span lang="it" xml:lang="it"><i>Repubblica Italiana</i></span>), is a country located in <a href="/wiki/Southern_Europe" title="Southern Europe">Southern Europe</a>, occupying the <a href="/wiki/Italian_Peninsula" title="Italian Peninsula">Italian Peninsula</a>, and the two largest islands in the Mediterranean Sea, <a href="/wiki/Sicily" title="Sicily">Sicily</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sardinia" title="Sardinia">Sardinia</a>. Italy shares its northern <a href="/wiki/Alpine" title="Alpine">alpine</a> boundary with <a href="/wiki/France" title="France">France</a>, <a href="/wiki/Switzerland" title="Switzerland">Switzerland</a>, <a href="/wiki/Austria" title="Austria">Austria</a> and <a href="/wiki/Slovenia" title="Slovenia">Slovenia</a>. The independent states of <a href="/wiki/San_Marino" title="San Marino">San Marino</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Vatican_City" title="Vatican City">Vatican City</a> are enclaves within Italian peninsula, while <a href="/wiki/Campione_d%27Italia" title="Campione d'Italia">Campione d'Italia</a> is an Italian exclave in Switzerland.</p>
<p>Italy has been the home of many influential European cultures, such as the <a href="/wiki/Etruscans" title="Etruscans">Etruscans</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Romans" title="Romans">Romans</a> and much later the <a href="/wiki/Italian_Renaissance" title="Italian Renaissance">Italian Renaissance</a>, as well as a host to 3,000 years of <a href="/wiki/Migrations" title="Migrations">migrations</a> and <a href="/wiki/Invasions" title="Invasions">invasions</a> from <a href="/wiki/Germanic" title="Germanic">Germanic</a>, <a href="/wiki/Celt" title="Celt">Celtic</a>, <a href="/wiki/Greek" title="Greek">Greek</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lombard" title="Lombard">Lombard</a>, <a href="/wiki/Saracen" title="Saracen">Saracen</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Norman" title="Norman">Norman</a> peoples. Italy's capital <a href="/wiki/Rome" title="Rome">Rome</a> has historically been an important centre of <a href="/wiki/Western_Civilization" title="Western Civilization">Western Civilization</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Roman_Catholic_Church" title="Roman Catholic Church">Roman Catholic Church</a>.</p>
<p>Today, Italy is a democratic republic, and a <a href="/wiki/Developed_country" title="Developed country">developed country</a> with the <a href="/wiki/List_of_countries_by_GDP_%28nominal%29" title="List of countries by GDP (nominal)">7th-highest</a> <a href="/wiki/Gross_domestic_product" title="Gross domestic product">GDP</a> and the 17th-highest <a href="/wiki/Human_Development_Index" title="Human Development Index">Human Development Index</a> rating in the world. It is a member of the <a href="/wiki/G8" title="G8">G8</a> and a founding member of what is now the <a href="/wiki/European_Union" title="European Union">European Union</a> (having signed the <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Rome" title="Treaty of Rome">Treaty of Rome</a> in 1957), of the <a href="/wiki/Council_of_Europe" title="Council of Europe">Council of Europe</a> and of the <a href="/wiki/Western_European_Union" title="Western European Union">Western European Union</a> and of the <a href="/wiki/Central_European_Initiative" title="Central European Initiative">Central European Initiative</a>. Starting from <a href="/wiki/January_1" title="January 1">January 1</a>, <a href="/wiki/2007" title="2007">2007</a>, Italy is a <a href="/wiki/List_of_elected_members_of_the_United_Nations_Security_Council" title="List of elected members of the United Nations Security Council">non-permanent member</a> of the <a href="/wiki/United_Nations_Security_Council" title="United Nations Security Council">United Nations Security Council</a>. It is considered by some a <a href="/wiki/Great_power" title="Great power">great power</a>.</p>

<p><a name="Etymology" id="Etymology"></a></p>
<h2><span class="editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=Italy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Etymology">edit</a>]</span> <span class="mw-headline">Etymology</span></h2>
<p>The origin of the term "Italy" (It: <i>Italia</i>), from the Latin <i>Ītalia</i>, is uncertain and different ones have been proposed. According to one of the more common explications the term would have been borrowed through <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greek" title="Ancient Greek">Greek</a>, from <a href="/wiki/Oscan" title="Oscan">Oscan</a> <i>Víteliú</i>. So it would mean "the land of the cattle" and it would name for the god of cattle, <a href="/wiki/Mars_%28mythology%29" title="Mars (mythology)">Mars</a><sup id="_ref-1" class="reference"><a href="#_note-1" title="">[2]</a></sup>. The bull was a symbol of the southern Italian tribes and is often depicted goring the Roman wolf as a defiant symbol of free Italy during the <a href="/wiki/Samnite" title="Samnite">Samnite</a> wars.</p>
<p>The name <i>Italia</i> applied to a part of what is now <a href="/wiki/Southern_Italy" title="Southern Italy">southern Italy</a>. According to <a href="/wiki/Antiochus_of_Syracuse" title="Antiochus of Syracuse">Antiochus of Syracuse</a>, it originally referred to only the southern portion of the <a href="/wiki/Bruttium" title="Bruttium">Bruttium</a> peninsula (modern <a href="/wiki/Calabria" title="Calabria">Calabria</a>), but by his time <a href="/wiki/Oenotrians" title="Oenotrians">Oenotria</a> and Italy had become synonymous, and the name also applied to most of <a href="/wiki/Lucania" title="Lucania">Lucania</a> as well.</p>
<p><a name="History" id="History"></a></p>
<h2><span class="editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=Italy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: History">edit</a>]</span> <span class="mw-headline">History</span></h2>
<dl>
<dd>
<div class="noprint"><i>Main article: <a href="/wiki/History_of_Italy" title="History of Italy">History of Italy</a></i></div>
</dd>
</dl>
<p><a name="Prehistory_to_Magna_Grecia" id="Prehistory_to_Magna_Grecia"></a></p>
<h3><span class="editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=Italy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Prehistory to Magna Grecia">edit</a>]</span> <span class="mw-headline">Prehistory to Magna Grecia</span></h3>
<p>Excavations throughout Italy have unearthed proof of human presence in Italy dating back to the <a href="/wiki/Palaeolithic" title="Palaeolithic">Palaeolithic</a> period (the "Old Stone Age") some 200,000 years ago. In the 8th and 7th centuries BC, driven by unsettled conditions at home, <a href="/wiki/Greek_colonies" title="Greek colonies">Greek colonies</a> were established in places as widely separated as the eastern coast of the <a href="/wiki/Black_Sea" title="Black Sea">Black Sea</a> and <a href="/wiki/Marseille" title="Marseille">Massilia</a> (what is now <a href="/wiki/Marseille" title="Marseille">Marseille</a>, <a href="/wiki/France" title="France">France</a>). They included settlements in <a href="/wiki/Sicily" title="Sicily">Sicily</a> and the southern part of the Italian peninsula. The Romans called the area of Sicily and the foot of the boot of Italy <i><a href="/wiki/Magna_Graecia" title="Magna Graecia">Magna Graecia</a></i> (Latin, “Greater Greece”), since it was so densely inhabited by <a href="/wiki/Greeks" title="Greeks">Greeks</a>.</p>
<p><a name="Ancient_Rome" id="Ancient_Rome"></a></p>
<h3><span class="editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=Italy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Ancient Rome">edit</a>]</span> <span class="mw-headline">Ancient Rome</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a href="/wiki/Image:Colosseum_in_Rome%2C_Italy_-_April_2007.jpg" class="internal" title="The Colosseum in Rome, perhaps the most enduring symbol of Italy"><img alt="The Colosseum in Rome, perhaps the most enduring symbol of Italy" longdesc="/wiki/Image:Colosseum_in_Rome%2C_Italy_-_April_2007.jpg" class="thumbimage" src="http://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/Colosseum_in_Rome%2C_Italy_-_April_2007.jpg/250px-Colosseum_in_Rome%2C_Italy_-_April_2007.jpg" width="250" height="147" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify" style="float:right"><a href="/wiki/Image:Colosseum_in_Rome%2C_Italy_-_April_2007.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"><img src="/skins-1.5/common/images/magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" alt="" /></a></div>
The <a href="/wiki/Colosseum" title="Colosseum">Colosseum</a> in Rome, perhaps the most enduring symbol of Italy</div>
</div>
</div>
<dl>
<dd>
<div class="noprint"><i>Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Rome" title="Ancient Rome">Ancient Rome</a> and <a href="/wiki/Italia_%28Roman_Empire%29" title="Italia (Roman Empire)">Italia (Roman Empire)</a></i></div>
</dd>
</dl>
<p>Ancient <a href="/wiki/Rome" title="Rome">Rome</a> was a <a href="/wiki/Civilization" title="Civilization">civilization</a> that grew from a small agricultural community founded on the <a href="/wiki/Italian_Peninsula" title="Italian Peninsula">Italian Peninsula</a> circa the 9th century BC to a massive <a href="/wiki/Empire" title="Empire">empire</a> straddling the <a href="/wiki/Mediterranean_Sea" title="Mediterranean Sea">Mediterranean Sea</a>. In its twelve-century existence, Roman civilization shifted from a <a href="/wiki/Monarchy" title="Monarchy">monarchy</a>, to a <a href="/wiki/Roman_Republic" title="Roman Republic">republic</a> based on a combination of <a href="/wiki/Oligarchy" title="Oligarchy">oligarchy</a> and <a href="/wiki/Democracy" title="Democracy">democracy</a>, to an <a href="/wiki/Autocracy" title="Autocracy">autocratic</a> <a href="/wiki/Roman_Empire" title="Roman Empire">empire</a>. It came to dominate <a href="/wiki/Western_Europe" title="Western Europe">Western Europe</a> and the entire area surrounding the <a href="/wiki/Mediterranean_Sea" title="Mediterranean Sea">Mediterranean Sea</a> through <a href="/wiki/Invasion" title="Invasion">conquest</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cultural_assimilation" title="Cultural assimilation">assimilation</a>.</p>
<p><b>Italia</b>, under the <a href="/wiki/Roman_Republic" title="Roman Republic">Roman Republic</a> and later <a href="/wiki/Roman_Empire" title="Roman Empire">Empire</a>, was the name of the <a href="/wiki/Italian_peninsula" title="Italian peninsula">Italian peninsula</a>. During the Republic, Italia (which extended at the time from <a href="/wiki/Rubicon" title="Rubicon">Rubicon</a> to <a href="/wiki/Calabria" title="Calabria">Calabria</a>) was not a <a href="/wiki/Roman_province" title="Roman province">province</a>, but rather the territory of the city of <a href="/wiki/Rome" title="Rome">Rome</a>, thus having a special status: for example, military commanders were not allowed to bring their armies within Italia, and <a href="/wiki/Julius_Caesar" title="Julius Caesar">Julius Caesar</a> passing the Rubicon with his legions marked the start of the civil war.</p>
<p>From the 3rd century, the Roman empire went into decline. The <a href="/wiki/Western_Roman_Empire" title="Western Roman Empire">western half of the empire</a>, including <a href="/wiki/Hispania" title="Hispania">Hispania</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gaul" title="Gaul">Gaul</a>, and Italy, broke into independent kingdoms in the 5th century. The eastern empire, governed from <a href="/wiki/Constantinople" title="Constantinople">Constantinople</a>, is usually referred to as the <a href="/wiki/Byzantine_Empire" title="Byzantine Empire">Byzantine Empire</a> after 476, the traditional date for the "fall of Rome" and for the subsequent onset of the <a href="/wiki/Early_Middle_Ages" title="Early Middle Ages">Early Middle Ages</a>, also known as the <a href="/wiki/Dark_Ages" title="Dark Ages">Dark Ages</a>.</p>
<p><a name="From_Dark_Ages_to_Renaissance" id="From_Dark_Ages_to_Renaissance"></a></p>
<h3><span class="editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=Italy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: From Dark Ages to Renaissance">edit</a>]</span> <span class="mw-headline">From Dark Ages to Renaissance</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:152px;"><a href="/wiki/Image:Leonardo_self.jpg" class="internal" title="Leonardo Da Vinci, Italian Renaissance man."><img alt="Leonardo Da Vinci, Italian Renaissance man." longdesc="/wiki/Image:Leonardo_self.jpg" class="thumbimage" src="http://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/ba/Leonardo_self.jpg/150px-Leonardo_self.jpg" width="150" height="235" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify" style="float:right"><a href="/wiki/Image:Leonardo_self.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"><img src="/skins-1.5/common/images/magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" alt="" /></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Leonardo_Da_Vinci" title="Leonardo Da Vinci">Leonardo Da Vinci</a>, Italian Renaissance man.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Population and economy started to slowly pick up after 1000, with the resurgence of cities (which organized themselves politically in <i><a href="/wiki/Medieval_commune" title="Medieval commune">Comuni</a></i>), trade, arts and literature.</p>
<p>During the later <a href="/wiki/Middle_Ages" title="Middle Ages">Middle Ages</a> the partially democratic Comuni, which could not face the challenges of that period, were substituted by monarchic-absolutistic governments (<i><a href="/wiki/Signoria" title="Signoria">Signorie</a></i>), but the fragmentation of the peninsula, especially in the northern and central parts of the country, continued, while the southern part, with <a href="/wiki/Naples" title="Naples">Naples</a>, <a href="/wiki/Apulia" title="Apulia">Apulia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sicily" title="Sicily">Sicily</a>, remained under a single domination. <a href="/wiki/Venice" title="Venice">Venice</a> and <a href="/wiki/Genoa" title="Genoa">Genoa</a> created powerful commercial empires in the Eastern part of the <a href="/wiki/Mediterranean_Sea" title="Mediterranean Sea">Mediterranean Sea</a> and <a href="/wiki/Black_Sea" title="Black Sea">Black Sea</a>.</p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Black_Death" title="Black Death">Black Death</a> in 1348 inflicted a terrible blow to Italy, resulting in one third of the population killed by the disease. The recovery from the disaster led to a new resurgence of cities, trade and economy which greatly stimulated the successive phase of the <a href="/wiki/Humanism" title="Humanism">Humanism</a> and <a href="/wiki/Renaissance" title="Renaissance">Renaissance</a> (15th-16th centuries) when Italy again returned to be the centre of <a href="/wiki/Western_civilization" title="Western civilization">Western civilization</a>, strongly influencing the other European countries. During this period the many Signorie gathered in a small number of regional states, but none of them had enough power to unify the peninsula.</p>
<p>After a century where the fragmented system of Italian states and principalities were able to maintain a relative independence and a balance of power in the peninsula, in 1494 the French king <a href="/wiki/Charles_VIII_of_France" title="Charles VIII of France">Charles VIII</a> opened the first of a series of invasions, lasting half of the <a href="/wiki/16th_century" title="16th century">sixteenth century</a>, and a competition between <a href="/wiki/France" title="France">France</a> and <a href="/wiki/Spain" title="Spain">Spain</a> for the possession of the country. Ultimately Spain prevailed (the <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Cateau-Cambresis" title="Treaty of Cateau-Cambresis">Treaty of Cateau-Cambresis</a> in 1559 recognised the Spanish possession of the <a href="/wiki/Duchy_of_Milan" title="Duchy of Milan">Duchy of Milan</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Naples" title="Kingdom of Naples">Kingdom of Naples</a>) and for almost two centuries became the hegemon in Italy. The holy alliance between <a href="/wiki/Habsburg_Spain" title="Habsburg Spain">Habsburg Spain</a> and the Holy See resulted in the systematic persecution of any Protestant movement, with the result that Italy remained a Catholic country with marginal Protestant presence.</p>
<p>The coastal villages and towns of Italy, like <a href="/wiki/Civezza" title="Civezza">Civezza</a> or <a href="/wiki/Imperia" title="Imperia">Imperia</a>, and <a href="/wiki/List_of_islands_in_the_Mediterranean" title="List of islands in the Mediterranean">Mediterranean islands</a> were frequently attacked by <a href="/wiki/Barbary_pirates" title="Barbary pirates">Barbary pirates</a> from North Africa and long stretches of the Italian coasts were almost completely abandoned by their inhabitants. According to Robert Davis between 1 million and 1.25 million Europeans were captured by <a href="/wiki/North_African" title="North African">North African</a> pirates and sold as <a href="/wiki/Arab_slave_trade" title="Arab slave trade">slaves</a> between the 16th and 19th century. These slaves were captured mainly from seaside villages in <a href="/wiki/Spain" title="Spain">Spain</a> and Italy.</p>
<p><a name="Kingdom_of_Sardinia_and_the_struggle_for_unification" id="Kingdom_of_Sardinia_and_the_struggle_for_unification"></a></p>
<h3><span class="editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=Italy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Kingdom of Sardinia and the struggle for unification">edit</a>]</span> <span class="mw-headline">Kingdom of Sardinia and the struggle for unification</span></h3>
<dl>
<dd>
<div class="noprint"><i>Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Sardinia" title="Kingdom of Sardinia">Kingdom of Sardinia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Risorgimento" title="Risorgimento">Risorgimento</a></i></div>
</dd>
</dl>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:152px;"><a href="/wiki/Image:Giuseppe_Garibaldi_%281866%29.jpg" class="internal" title="Giuseppe Garibaldi, the &quot;Hero of the Two Worlds&quot;"><img alt="Giuseppe Garibaldi, the &quot;Hero of the Two Worlds&quot;" longdesc="/wiki/Image:Giuseppe_Garibaldi_%281866%29.jpg" class="thumbimage" src="http://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/3/3a/Giuseppe_Garibaldi_%281866%29.jpg/150px-Giuseppe_Garibaldi_%281866%29.jpg" width="150" height="193" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify" style="float:right"><a href="/wiki/Image:Giuseppe_Garibaldi_%281866%29.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"><img src="/skins-1.5/common/images/magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" alt="" /></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Giuseppe_Garibaldi" title="Giuseppe Garibaldi">Giuseppe Garibaldi</a>, the "Hero of the Two Worlds"</div>
</div>
</div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Austria" title="Austria">Austria</a> succeeded Spain as Hegemon in Italy after the <a href="/wiki/Peace_of_Utrecht" title="Peace of Utrecht">Peace of Utrecht</a> (1713), having acquired the State of <a href="/wiki/Milan" title="Milan">Milan</a> and the Kingdom of Naples. The Austrian domination, thanks to the <a href="/wiki/Age_of_Enlightenment" title="Age of Enlightenment">Enlightenment</a> embraced by <a href="/wiki/Habsburg" title="Habsburg">Habsburgic emperors</a>, was a considerable improvement. The northern part of Italy, under the direct control of <a href="/wiki/Vienna" title="Vienna">Vienna</a>,gained economic dynamism and intellectual fervour, which improved its situation.</p>
<p>In 1720 the kingdom of Sicily was exchanged for that of Sardinia, and the House of Savoy was enabled to call itself royal, as Kings of Sardinia. Officially, the nation's name became "Kingdom of Sardinia, Cyprus, and Jerusalem, Duchy of Savoy and Montferrat, Principality of Piedmont." During most of the 18th- and 19th century under the House of Savoy, the political and economical capital was <a href="/wiki/Turin" title="Turin">Turin</a>.</p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/French_Revolution" title="French Revolution">French Revolution</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Napoleonic_War" title="Napoleonic War">Napoleonic War</a> (1796-1815) introduced the modern ideas of <a href="/wiki/Egalitarianism" title="Egalitarianism">equality</a>, <a href="/wiki/Democracy" title="Democracy">democracy</a>, <a href="/wiki/Law" title="Law">law</a> and <a href="/wiki/Nation" title="Nation">nation</a>. The peninsula was not a main battle field as in the past but <a href="/wiki/Napoleon_I_of_France" title="Napoleon I of France">Napoleon</a> (born in <a href="/wiki/Corsica" title="Corsica">Corsica</a> in 1769, one year after the cession of the island from Genoa to France) changed completely its political map, destroying in 1799 the <a href="/wiki/Republic_of_Venice" title="Republic of Venice">Republic of Venice</a>, which never recovered its independence. The states founded by Napoleon with the support of minority groups of Italian patriots were short-lived and did not survive the defeat of the French Emperor in 1815.</p>
<p>In 1814 the kingdom was restored and enlarged with the addition of the former Republic of Genoa, now a duchy, and it served as a buffer state against France. In the reaction after Napoleon, the country was ruled by conservative monarchs: Victor Emmanuel I and Charles Albert, who fought at the head of a contingent of his own troops at the Battle of Trocadero, which set the reactionary Ferdinand VII on the Spanish throne.</p>
<p>The Kingdom of Sardinia industrialized from 1830 onward. A constitution, the Statuto Albertino was enacted in the year of revolutions, 1848, under liberal pressure, and under the same pressure war was declared on Austria. After initial success the war took a turn for the worse and the Kingdom of Sardinia lost.</p>
<p>Like all of Italy, the Kingdom of Sardinia was troubled with political instability, under alternating governments. After a very short and disastrous second war with Austria, Charles Albert abdicated on March 23, 1849, in favour of his son Victor Emmanuel II. In 1850 a liberal ministry under Count Camillo Benso di Cavour was installed, and the Kingdom of Sardinia became the engine driving the Italian Unification. The Kingdom of Sardinia took part in the Crimean War, allied with Turkey, Britain and France, and fighting against Russia.</p>
<p><a name="Kingdom_of_Italy_to_Fascism_.281861-1922.29" id="Kingdom_of_Italy_to_Fascism_.281861-1922.29"></a></p>
<h3><span class="editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=Italy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Kingdom of Italy to Fascism (1861-1922)">edit</a>]</span> <span class="mw-headline">Kingdom of Italy to Fascism (1861-1922)</span></h3>
<dl>
<dd>
<div class="noprint"><i>Main article: <a href="/wiki/History_of_Italy_as_a_monarchy_and_in_the_World_Wars" title="History of Italy as a monarchy and in the World Wars">History of Italy as a monarchy and in the World Wars</a></i></div>
</dd>
</dl>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:152px;"><a href="/wiki/Image:Benito_Mussolini_1.jpg" class="internal" title="Benito Mussolini, &quot;Il Duce&quot;"><img alt="Benito Mussolini, &quot;Il Duce&quot;" longdesc="/wiki/Image:Benito_Mussolini_1.jpg" class="thumbimage" src="http://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/e/ee/Benito_Mussolini_1.jpg/150px-Benito_Mussolini_1.jpg" width="150" height="303" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify" style="float:right"><a href="/wiki/Image:Benito_Mussolini_1.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"><img src="/skins-1.5/common/images/magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" alt="" /></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Benito_Mussolini" title="Benito Mussolini">Benito Mussolini</a>, "Il Duce"</div>
</div>
</div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Industrialisation" title="Industrialisation">Industrialisation</a> and <a href="/wiki/Modernisation" title="Modernisation">modernisation</a>, at least in <a href="/wiki/Northern_Italy" title="Northern Italy">northern Italy</a>, started in the last part of the <a href="/wiki/19th_century" title="19th century">nineteenth century</a> under a protectionist regime. The <a href="/wiki/Mezzogiorno" title="Mezzogiorno">south</a>, in the meanwhile, stagnated under overpopulation and underdevelopment, forcing millions of people to search for employment and better conditions of life abroad. This <a href="/wiki/Italian_diaspora" title="Italian diaspora">diaspora</a> lasted until about 1970. It is calculated that more than 26 million Italians migrated to other European countries (primarily <a href="/wiki/France" title="France">France</a>, <a href="/wiki/Switzerland" title="Switzerland">Switzerland</a>, <a href="/wiki/Germany" title="Germany">Germany</a>, <a href="/wiki/Belgium" title="Belgium">Belgium</a> and <a href="/wiki/Luxembourg" title="Luxembourg">Luxembourg</a>), the Americas (especially to <a href="/wiki/Argentina" title="Argentina">Argentina</a>, <a href="/wiki/Brazil" title="Brazil">Brazil</a>, <a href="/wiki/Canada" title="Canada">Canada</a> and the <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">United States</a>), and <a href="/wiki/Australia" title="Australia">Australia</a>.</p>
<p>Parliamentary democracy developed considerably at the beginning of the <a href="/wiki/20th_century" title="20th century">twentieth century</a>. The Sardinian <a href="/wiki/Statuto_Albertino" title="Statuto Albertino">Statuto Albertino</a> of 1848, extended to the whole <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Italy_%281861-1946%29" title="Kingdom of Italy (1861-1946)">Kingdom of Italy</a> in 1861, provided for basic freedoms, but the electoral laws excluded the non-propertied and uneducated classes from voting. In 1913 male universal suffrage was allowed. The <a href="/wiki/Socialist" title="Socialist">Socialist</a> Party resulted the main political party, outclassing the traditional liberal and conservative organisations. The path to a modern liberal democracy was interrupted by the tragedy of the <a href="/wiki/World_War_I" title="World War I">First World War</a> (1914-1918), which Italy fought along with France and the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_of_Great_Britain_and_Ireland" title="United Kingdom of Great Britain and Ireland">United Kingdom</a>. Italy was able to beat the <a href="/wiki/Austrian-Hungarian_Empire" title="Austrian-Hungarian Empire">Austrian-Hungarian Empire</a> in November 1918. It obtained <a href="/wiki/Autonomous_Province_of_Trento" title="Autonomous Province of Trento">Trentino</a>, <a href="/wiki/Autonomous_Province_of_Bolzano-Bozen" title="Autonomous Province of Bolzano-Bozen">Alto Adige/Südtirol</a>, <a href="/wiki/Trieste" title="Trieste">Trieste</a> and <a href="/wiki/Istria" title="Istria">Istria</a>, besides <a href="/wiki/Rijeka" title="Rijeka">Fiume</a> and a few territories on the <a href="/wiki/Dalmatia" title="Dalmatia">Dalmatian coast</a> (<a href="/wiki/Zadar" title="Zadar">Zara</a>), gaining respect as an international power, but the population had to pay a heavy human and social price. The war produced more than 600,000 dead, <a href="/wiki/Inflation" title="Inflation">inflation</a> and <a href="/wiki/Unemployment" title="Unemployment">unemployment</a>, economic and political instability, which in the end favoured the <a href="/wiki/Italian_fascism" title="Italian fascism">Fascist</a> movement to violently seize power in 1922, albeit with the support of the King <a href="/wiki/Vittorio_Emanuele_III" title="Vittorio Emanuele III">Vittorio Emanuele III</a>, who feared <a href="/wiki/Civil_war" title="Civil war">civil war</a> and <a href="/wiki/Revolution" title="Revolution">revolution</a>, and preserving, at least initially, constitutional procedures.</p>
<p><a name="Fascism_and_World_War_II_.281922-1945.29" id="Fascism_and_World_War_II_.281922-1945.29"></a></p>
<h3><span class="editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=Italy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Fascism and World War II (1922-1945)">edit</a>]</span> <span class="mw-headline">Fascism and World War II (1922-1945)</span></h3>
<p>The fascist dictatorship of <a href="/wiki/Benito_Mussolini" title="Benito Mussolini">Benito Mussolini</a> lasted from 1922 to 1943 but in the first years Mussolini maintained the appearance of a liberal democracy. After rigged elections in 1924 gave to Fascism and its conservative allies an absolute majority in <a href="/wiki/Parliament" title="Parliament">Parliament</a>, Mussolini cancelled all democratic liberties on <a href="/wiki/January_3" title="January 3">January 3</a>, <a href="/wiki/1925" title="1925">1925</a>.</p>
<p>In 1935 Mussolini declared war on <a href="/wiki/Ethiopia" title="Ethiopia">Ethiopia</a> on a territorial pretext. Ethiopia was subjugated in a few months. This resulted in the alienation of Italy from its traditional allies, France and the United Kingdom, and it's nearing to Nazi Germany. A first pact with Germany was concluded in 1936 and then in 1938 (the <a href="/wiki/Pact_of_Steel" title="Pact of Steel">Pact of Steel</a>). Italy supported Franco's revolution in the Spanish civil war and <a href="/wiki/Adolf_Hitler" title="Adolf Hitler">Hitler</a>'s pretensions in central Europe, accepting the annexation of Austria to Germany in 1938, although the disappearance of a buffer state between mighty Germany and Italy was unfavourable for the country. In October 1938 Mussolini managed to avoid imminent eruption of another war in Europe, bringing together the United Kingdom, France and Germany <a href="/wiki/Munich_Agreement" title="Munich Agreement">at the expense</a> of <a href="/wiki/Czechoslovakia" title="Czechoslovakia">Czechoslovakia</a>'s integrity.</p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a href="/wiki/Image:Italian_empire_1940.PNG" class="internal" title="The Italian empire in 1940"><img alt="The Italian empire in 1940" longdesc="/wiki/Image:Italian_empire_1940.PNG" class="thumbimage" src="http://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/39/Italian_empire_1940.PNG/200px-Italian_empire_1940.PNG" width="200" height="140" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify" style="float:right"><a href="/wiki/Image:Italian_empire_1940.PNG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"><img src="/skins-1.5/common/images/magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" alt="" /></a></div>
The <a href="/wiki/Italian_empire" title="Italian empire">Italian empire</a> in 1940</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In April 1939 Italy occupied <a href="/wiki/Albania" title="Albania">Albania</a>, a <i>de-facto</i> protectorate for decades, but in September 1939, after the invasion of Poland, Mussolini decided not to intervene on Germany's side, due to the poor preparation of the armed forces. Italy entered the war in June 1940 when France was almost defeated. Mussolini hoped for a quick victory but Italy showed from the very beginning the poor nature of its army and the scarce ability of its generals. Italy invaded Greece in October 1940 via Albania but after a few days was forced to withdraw. After conquering British Somalia in 1940, a counter-attack by the Allies led to the loss of the whole <a href="/wiki/Italian_empire" title="Italian empire">Italian empire</a> in the Horn of Africa. Italy was also defeated by Allied forces in North Africa and was saved only by the German armed forces led by <a href="/wiki/Erwin_Rommel" title="Erwin Rommel">Erwin Rommel</a>.</p>
<p>After several defeats, Italy was invaded in June 1943. In July 1943 King Vittorio Emanuele III and a group of Fascist leaders staged a coup d'etat against Mussolini, having him arrested. While the old pre-Fascist political parties resurfaced, secret peace negotiations with the Allies were started. In September 1943 Italy surrendered. It was immediately invaded by Germany and for nearly two years the country was divided and became a battlefield. The Nazi-occupied part of the country, where a <a href="/wiki/Italian_Social_Republic" title="Italian Social Republic">fascist state</a> under Mussolini was reconstituted, was the theatre of a savage civil war between <a href="/wiki/Italian_partisans" title="Italian partisans">Italian partisans</a> (<i>"<a href="/wiki/Italian_resistance_movement" title="Italian resistance movement">partigiani</a>"</i>) and Nazi and fascist troops. The country was liberated by a national uprising on <a href="/wiki/April_25" title="April 25">25 April</a> <a href="/wiki/1945" title="1945">1945</a> (the <i><a href="/wiki/Liberazione" title="Liberazione">Liberazione</a></i>).</p>
<p><a name="The_First_Republic_.281946-1992.29" id="The_First_Republic_.281946-1992.29"></a></p>
<h3><span class="editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=Italy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: The First Republic (1946-1992)">edit</a>]</span> <span class="mw-headline">The First Republic (1946-1992)</span></h3>
<dl>
<dd>
<div class="noprint"><i>Main article: <a href="/wiki/History_of_the_Italian_Republic" title="History of the Italian Republic">History of the Italian Republic</a></i></div>
</dd>
</dl>
<p>Under the <a href="/wiki/Paris_Peace_Treaties" title="Paris Peace Treaties">Paris Peace Treaties</a> of 1947, minor adjustments were made to Italy's frontier with France, the eastern border area was transferred to <a href="/wiki/Socialist_Federal_Republic_of_Yugoslavia" title="Socialist Federal Republic of Yugoslavia">Yugoslavia</a>, and the area around the city of <a href="/wiki/Trieste" title="Trieste">Trieste</a> was designated a free territory. In 1954, the free territory, which had remained under the administration of U.S.–UK forces (Zone A, including the city of Trieste) and Yugoslav forces (Zone B), was divided between Italy and Yugoslavia, principally along the zonal boundary.</p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:152px;"><a href="/wiki/Image:Moro_br_1.jpg" class="internal" title="Aldo Moro, photographed during his kidnapping by the BR."><img alt="Aldo Moro, photographed during his kidnapping by the BR." longdesc="/wiki/Image:Moro_br_1.jpg" class="thumbimage" src="http://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/0/01/Moro_br_1.jpg/150px-Moro_br_1.jpg" width="150" height="176" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify" style="float:right"><a href="/wiki/Image:Moro_br_1.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"><img src="/skins-1.5/common/images/magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" alt="" /></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Aldo_Moro" title="Aldo Moro">Aldo Moro</a>, photographed during his kidnapping by the <a href="/wiki/Red_Brigades" title="Red Brigades">BR.</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In 1946 <a href="/wiki/Vittorio_Emanuele_III" title="Vittorio Emanuele III">Vittorio Emanuele III</a> abdicated the throne in favor of his son <a href="/wiki/Umberto_II" title="Umberto II">Umberto II</a> who again faced the possibility of civil war. <a href="/wiki/Birth_of_the_Italian_Republic" title="Birth of the Italian Republic">Italy became a Republic</a> after the result of a popular <a href="/wiki/Referendum_in_Italy" title="Referendum in Italy">referendum</a> held on <a href="/wiki/June_2" title="June 2">2 June</a> <a href="/wiki/1946" title="1946">1946</a>, a day since then celebrated as <a href="/wiki/Republic_Day" title="Republic Day">Republic Day</a>. The republic won with a 9% margin; the north of Italy voted prevalently for a republic, the south for the monarchy. The Republican Constitution was approved and entered into force on <a href="/wiki/January_1" title="January 1">1 January</a> <a href="/wiki/1948" title="1948">1948</a>, including a provisional measure banning all male members of the House of Savoy from Italy. This stipulation was redressed in 2002.</p>
<p>In 1949 Italy became a member of the <a href="/wiki/NATO" title="NATO">NATO</a> alliance and an ally of the United States, which helped to revive the Italian economy through the <a href="/wiki/Marshall_Plan" title="Marshall Plan">Marshall Plan</a>. In the same years, Italy also became a member of the <a href="/wiki/European_Economic_Community" title="European Economic Community">European Economic Community</a> (EEC), which later transformed into the European Union (<a href="/wiki/EU" title="EU">EU</a>). In <a href="/wiki/%2750s" title="'50s">'50s</a> and <a href="/wiki/%2760s" title="'60s">'60s</a> the country enjoyed prolonged economic growth was termed "Economic Miracle", which lifted the country among the most industrialised nations in the world, with a perennial political instability.</p>
<p>Italy crossed a period of political turmoil in the 1970s, which progressively ended in the early 1980s. Known as the <a href="/wiki/History_of_Italy_%281970s-1980s%29" title="History of Italy (1970s-1980s)">Years of Lead</a>, this period was characterized by widespread social conflicts and terrorism acts carried out by extra-parliamentary movements. The assassination of the leader of the <a href="/wiki/Christian_Democracy_%28Italy%29" title="Christian Democracy (Italy)">Christian Democracy</a> (DC), <a href="/wiki/Aldo_Moro" title="Aldo Moro">Aldo Moro</a>, in 1978 by the Second <a href="/wiki/Red_Brigades" title="Red Brigades">Red Brigades</a> led by <a href="/wiki/Mario_Moretti" title="Mario Moretti">Mario Moretti</a>, put an end to the negotiations of a "<a href="/wiki/Historic_compromise" title="Historic compromise">historic compromise</a>" between the DC and the <a href="/wiki/Italian_Communist_Party" title="Italian Communist Party">Communist Party</a> (PCI). In 2000, a Parliament Commission report from the <a href="/wiki/Olive_Tree" title="Olive Tree">Olive Tree</a> left-of-center coalition concluded that the strategy of tension had been supported by the United States to <i>"stop the PCI, and to a certain degree also the PSI, from reaching executive power in the country".</i><sup id="_ref-2" class="reference"><a href="#_note-2" title="">[3]</a></sup><sup id="_ref-Fontana_0" class="reference"><a href="#_note-Fontana" title="">[4]</a></sup><sup id="_ref-Docs_0" class="reference"><a href="#_note-Docs" title="">[5]</a></sup></p>
<p>In the 1980s, for the first time, two governments were led by a republican and a socialist (<a href="/wiki/Bettino_Craxi" title="Bettino Craxi">Bettino Craxi</a>) rather than by a member of DC (which nonetheless remained the main force behind the government). With the end of the “lead years”, the PCI gradually increased their votes under the leadership of <a href="/wiki/Enrico_Berlinguer" title="Enrico Berlinguer">Enrico Berlinguer</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Italian_Socialist_Party" title="Italian Socialist Party">Socialist party</a> (PSI), led by <a href="/wiki/Bettino_Craxi" title="Bettino Craxi">Bettino Craxi</a>, became more and more critical of the communists and of the <a href="/wiki/Soviet_Union" title="Soviet Union">Soviet Union</a>; Craxi himself pushed in favour of US president <a href="/wiki/Ronald_Reagan" title="Ronald Reagan">Ronald Reagan</a>'s positioning of <a href="/wiki/MGM-31_Pershing" title="MGM-31 Pershing">Pershing</a> missiles in Italy.</p>
<p><a name="The_Second_Republic_.281992-present.29" id="The_Second_Republic_.281992-present.29"></a></p>
<h3><span class="editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=Italy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: The Second Republic (1992-present)">edit</a>]</span> <span class="mw-headline">The Second Republic (1992-present)</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:182px;"><a href="/wiki/Image:Craxi_coins.jpg" class="internal" title="Bettino Craxi, viewed by many as the symbol of Tangentopoli, leader of the Italian Socialist Party, is greeted by a salvo of coins as a sign of loathing by protesters contesting him."><img alt="Bettino Craxi, viewed by many as the symbol of Tangentopoli, leader of the Italian Socialist Party, is greeted by a salvo of coins as a sign of loathing by protesters contesting him." longdesc="/wiki/Image:Craxi_coins.jpg" class="thumbimage" src="http://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/b/b6/Craxi_coins.jpg/180px-Craxi_coins.jpg" width="180" height="138" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify" style="float:right"><a href="/wiki/Image:Craxi_coins.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"><img src="/skins-1.5/common/images/magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" alt="" /></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Bettino_Craxi" title="Bettino Craxi">Bettino Craxi</a>, viewed by many as the symbol of <a href="/wiki/Tangentopoli" title="Tangentopoli">Tangentopoli</a>, leader of the <a href="/wiki/Italian_Socialist_Party" title="Italian Socialist Party">Italian Socialist Party</a>, is greeted by a salvo of coins as a sign of loathing by protesters contesting him.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>From 1992 to 1997, Italy faced significant challenges as voters (disenchanted with past political paralysis, massive government debt, extensive corruption, and organized crime's considerable influence collectively called <a href="/wiki/Tangentopoli" title="Tangentopoli">Tangentopoli</a> after being uncovered by <a href="/wiki/Mani_pulite" title="Mani pulite">Mani pulite</a> - "Clean hands") demanded political, economic, and ethical reforms. The scandals involved all major parties, but especially those in the government coalition: between 1992 and 1994 the <a href="/wiki/Christian_Democracy_%28Italy%29" title="Christian Democracy (Italy)">DC</a> underwent a severe crisis and was dissolved, splitting up into several pieces, among whom the <a href="/w/index.php?title=Italian_People%E2%80%99s_Party&amp;action=edit" class="new" title="Italian People’s Party">Italian People’s Party</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Christian_Democratic_Center" title="Christian Democratic Center">Christian Democratic Center</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Italian_Socialist_Party" title="Italian Socialist Party">PSI</a> (and the other governing minor parties) completely dissolved.</p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:152px;"><a href="/wiki/Image:Silvio_Berlusconi_20040318a.jpg" class="internal" title="Silvio Berlusconi, Prime Minister of Italy in 1994 and from 2001 to 2006."><img alt="Silvio Berlusconi, Prime Minister of Italy in 1994 and from 2001 to 2006." longdesc="/wiki/Image:Silvio_Berlusconi_20040318a.jpg" class="thumbimage" src="http://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/26/Silvio_Berlusconi_20040318a.jpg/150px-Silvio_Berlusconi_20040318a.jpg" width="150" height="187" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify" style="float:right"><a href="/wiki/Image:Silvio_Berlusconi_20040318a.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"><img src="/skins-1.5/common/images/magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" alt="" /></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Silvio_Berlusconi" title="Silvio Berlusconi">Silvio Berlusconi</a>, Prime Minister of Italy in 1994 and from 2001 to 2006.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The 1994 elections also swept media magnate <a href="/wiki/Silvio_Berlusconi" title="Silvio Berlusconi">Silvio Berlusconi</a> (leader of "<a href="/wiki/Pole_of_Freedoms" title="Pole of Freedoms">Pole of Freedoms</a>" coalition) into office as Prime Minister. Berlusconi, however, was forced to step down in December 1994 when the <i>Lega Nord</i> withdrew support. The Berlusconi government was succeeded by a <a href="/wiki/Technical_government" title="Technical government">technical government</a> headed by Prime Minister <a href="/wiki/Lamberto_Dini" title="Lamberto Dini">Lamberto Dini</a>, which left office in early 1996.</p>
<p>In April 1996, national elections led to the victory of a center-left coalition under the leadership of <a href="/wiki/Romano_Prodi" title="Romano Prodi">Romano Prodi</a>. Prodi's first government became the third-longest to stay in power before he narrowly lost a vote of confidence, by one vote, in October 1998. A new government was formed by <a href="/wiki/Democrats_of_the_Left" title="Democrats of the Left">Democrats of the Left</a> leader and former communist <a href="/wiki/Massimo_D%27Alema" title="Massimo D'Alema">Massimo D'Alema</a>, but in April 2000, following poor performance by his coalition in regional elections, D'Alema resigned. The succeeding center-left government, including most of the same parties, was headed by <a href="/wiki/Giuliano_Amato" title="Giuliano Amato">Giuliano Amato</a> (social-democratic), who previously served as Prime Minister in 1992-93, from April 2000 until June 2001.</p>
<p>In 2001 the centre-right <a href="/wiki/Italian_general_election%2C_2001" title="Italian general election, 2001">formed the government</a> and <a href="/wiki/Silvio_Berlusconi" title="Silvio Berlusconi">Silvio Berlusconi</a> was able to remain in power for a complete five year mandate, became the longest government in post-war Italy. Berlusconi participated in the <a href="/wiki/Multinational_coalition_in_Iraq" title="Multinational coalition in Iraq">US-led military coalition in Iraq</a>, but his successor, Romano Prodi, withdrew Italian forces.</p>
<p>The last <a href="/wiki/Italian_general_election%2C_2006" title="Italian general election, 2006">elections in 2006</a> returned Prodi in the government with a slim majority. In the first year of his government, Prodi has followed a cautious policy of economic liberalization and reduction of public debt.</p>
<p><a name="Geography" id="Geography"></a></p>
<h2><span class="editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=Italy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Geography">edit</a>]</span> <span class="mw-headline">Geography</span></h2>
<dl>
<dd>
<div class="noprint"><i>Main article: <a href="/wiki/Geography_of_Italy" title="Geography of Italy">Geography of Italy</a></i></div>
</dd>
</dl>
<p><a name="Topography" id="Topography"></a></p>
<h3><span class="editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=Italy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Topography">edit</a>]</span> <span class="mw-headline">Topography</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:331px;"><a href="/wiki/Image:It-map.png" class="internal" title=""><img alt="" longdesc="/wiki/Image:It-map.png" class="thumbimage" src="http://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/d/da/It-map.png" width="329" height="353" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify" style="float:right"><a href="/wiki/Image:It-map.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"><img src="/skins-1.5/common/images/magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" alt="" /></a></div>
</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Italy, slightly larger than Arizona, is a long peninsula shaped like a boot, surrounded on the west by the Tyrrhenian Sea and on the east by the Adriatic. It is bounded by France, Switzerland, Austria, and Slovenia to the north. The Apennine Mountains form the peninsula's backbone; the Alps form its northern boundary. The largest of its many northern lakes is Garda (143 sq mi; 370 sq km); the Po, its principal river, flows from the Alps on Italy's western border and crosses the Lombard plain to the Adriatic Sea. Several islands form part of Italy; the largest are Sicily (9,926 sq mi; 25,708 sq km) and Sardinia (9,301 sq mi; 24,090 sq km).</p>
<p><a name="Climate" id="Climate"></a></p>
<h3><span class="editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=Italy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Climate">edit</a>]</span> <span class="mw-headline">Climate</span></h3>
<p>The climate in Italy is highly diverse and can be far from the stereotypical <a href="/wiki/Mediterranean_climate" title="Mediterranean climate">Mediterranean climate</a> and "land of <a href="/wiki/Sun" title="Sun">sun</a>", depending on the location. The inland northern areas of Italy (Turin, Milan, and Bologna) have a <a href="/wiki/Continental_climate" title="Continental climate">continental climate</a>, while the coastal areas of Liguria and the peninsula south of Florence fit the stereotype. The coastal areas of the peninsula can be very different from the interior, particularly during the winter months. The higher altitudes are cold, wet, and often snowy. The coastal regions, where most of the large towns are located, have a typical <a href="/wiki/Mediterranean" title="Mediterranean">Mediterranean</a> climate with mild winters and hot and generally dry summers. The length and intensity of the summer dry season increases southwards (compare the tables for <a href="/wiki/Rome" title="Rome">Rome</a>, <a href="/wiki/Naples" title="Naples">Naples</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Brindisi" title="Brindisi">Brindisi</a>).</p>
<p>Between the north and south there is a quite remarkable difference in the temperatures, above all during the winter: in some winter days it can be -2 °C and snowing in Milan while Rome gets +12°C and it is +18°C in <a href="/wiki/Palermo" title="Palermo">Palermo</a>. Temperature differences are less extreme in the summer.</p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a href="/wiki/Image:Montblanc.jpg" class="internal" title="Mont Blanc (Monte Bianco), the highest mountain in Italy and Western Europe."><img alt="Mont Blanc (Monte Bianco), the highest mountain in Italy and Western Europe." longdesc="/wiki/Image:Montblanc.jpg" class="thumbimage" src="http://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/60/Montblanc.jpg/250px-Montblanc.jpg" width="250" height="150" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify" style="float:right"><a href="/wiki/Image:Montblanc.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"><img src="/skins-1.5/common/images/magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" alt="" /></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Mont_Blanc" title="Mont Blanc">Mont Blanc</a> (Monte Bianco), the highest mountain in Italy and Western Europe.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The east coast of the <a href="/wiki/Peninsula" title="Peninsula">peninsula</a> is not as wet as the west coast, but is usually colder in the winter. The east coast north of <a href="/wiki/Pescara" title="Pescara">Pescara</a> is occasionally affected by the cold <a href="/wiki/Bora_%28wind%29" title="Bora (wind)">bora</a> winds in winter and spring, but the wind is less strong here than around <a href="/wiki/Trieste" title="Trieste">Trieste</a>. During these frosty spells from E-NE cities like Rimini, Ancona, Pescara and the entire eastern hillside of the Apennines can be affected by true "blizzards". The town of Fabriano, located just around 300 meters in elevation, can often see 0.50-0.60 m of fresh snow fall in 24 hours during these episodes. Northwide, on the coast line from Ravenna to Venice and Trieste, snow falls more rarely: during cold spells from east, the cold can be harsh but with bright skies; while, during the snowfalls that affects Northern Italy, on the Adriatic coast usually blows a milder Scirocco wind which makes snow turning into rain - the mild effects of this wind, anyway, often disappear just a few kilometers inside the plain, and sometime the coast from Venice to Grado sees snow while it is raining in Trieste, the Po mouths and Ravenna. Rarely, the city of Trieste may see snow blizzards with north-eastern winds, but just in very particular conditions; in the colder winters, the Venice Lagoon may freeze, and in the coldest ones even enough to walk on the ice sheet<a href="http://www.libreriasolaris.com/Venicepast/veniceonice.html" class="external autonumber" title="http://www.libreriasolaris.com/Venicepast/veniceonice.html" rel="nofollow">[5]</a>.</p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a href="/wiki/Image:Val-d%27Orcia-landscape-1.jpg" class="internal" title="Tuscany landscape."><img alt="Tuscany landscape." longdesc="/wiki/Image:Val-d%27Orcia-landscape-1.jpg" class="thumbimage" src="http://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9c/Val-d%27Orcia-landscape-1.jpg/250px-Val-d%27Orcia-landscape-1.jpg" width="250" height="188" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify" style="float:right"><a href="/wiki/Image:Val-d%27Orcia-landscape-1.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"><img src="/skins-1.5/common/images/magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" alt="" /></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Tuscany" title="Tuscany">Tuscany</a> landscape.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Italy is subject to highly different weather conditions in autumn, winter, and spring, while summer is usually more stable, although the northern regions often have thunderstorms in the afternoon/night hours and some grey and rainy days. So, while south of Florence the summer is typically dry and sunny, the north is tends to be more humid and cloudy. Spring and Autumn weather can be very changeable, with sunny and warm weeks (sometime with Summer-like temperatures) suddenly broken off by cold spells or followed by rainy and cloudy weeks.</p>
<p>The least number of rainy days and the highest number of hours of sunshine occur in the extreme south of the mainland and in <a href="/wiki/Sicily" title="Sicily">Sicily</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sardinia" title="Sardinia">Sardinia</a>. Here sunshine averages from four to five hours a day in winter and up to ten or eleven hours in summer. In the north precipitation is more evenly distributed during the year, although the summer is usually slightly wetter. Between November and March the Po valley is often covered by fog, especially in the central zone (Pavia, Cremona, and Mantua), while the number of days with lows below 0°C usually goes from 60 to 90 a year, with peaks of 100-110 days in the mainly rural zones<a href="http://sbegotti.altervista.org/immagini/Album/Galaverna301205/Galaverna301205.html" class="external autonumber" title="http://sbegotti.altervista.org/immagini/Album/Galaverna301205/Galaverna301205.html" rel="nofollow">[6]</a>. Snow is quite common between early December and early March in cities like Turin, Milan and Bologna, but sometime it appears in late November or late March and even April. In the winter of 2005-2006, Milan received around 0.75-0.80 m of fresh snow, Como around 1.00 m, Brescia 0.50 m, Trento 1.60 m, Vicenza around 0.45 m, Bologna around 0.30 m, and Piacenza around 0.80 m<a href="http://www.centrometeolombardo.com/vecchiosito/foto/nevicate/2006/brianzagrignagen06/brianzagrignagen06.htm" class="external autonumber" title="http://www.centrometeolombardo.com/vecchiosito/foto/nevicate/2006/brianzagrignagen06/brianzagrignagen06.htm" rel="nofollow">[7]</a>.</p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a href="/wiki/Image:Riva_del_Garda.jpg" class="internal" title="Lake Garda from Riva del Garda."><img alt="Lake Garda from Riva del Garda." longdesc="/wiki/Image:Riva_del_Garda.jpg" class="thumbimage" src="http://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6b/Riva_del_Garda.jpg/250px-Riva_del_Garda.jpg" width="250" height="188" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify" style="float:right"><a href="/wiki/Image:Riva_del_Garda.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"><img src="/skins-1.5/common/images/magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" alt="" /></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Lake_Garda" title="Lake Garda">Lake Garda</a> from <a href="/wiki/Riva_del_Garda" title="Riva del Garda">Riva del Garda</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Summer temperatures are often similar North to South, but with the different weather conditions seen above. July temperatures are 22-24 °C north of river Po, like in <a href="/wiki/Milan" title="Milan">Milan</a> or <a href="/wiki/Venice" title="Venice">Venice</a>, and south of river Po can reach 24-25 °C like in Bologna, with fewer thunderstorms; on the coasts of Central and Southern Italy, and in the near plains, mean temperatures goes from 23 °C to 27 °C. Generally, the hottest month is August in the south and July in the north; during these months the thermometer can reach 38-42 °C in the south and 32-35 °C in the north; Sometimes the country can be split as during winter, with rain and fresh temperatures like 20-22 °C during the day in the North, and 30 °C to 40 °C in the South; but, having a hot and dry summer does not mean that Southern Italy never see rain from June to August.</p>
<p>The coldest month is January: the Po valley's mean temperature is between -1°C and +1°C, Venice +2°/+3°C, Trieste +4°C, Florence 5°/6°C, Rome 7°/8°C, Naples 9°C, <a href="/wiki/Palermo" title="Palermo">Palermo</a> 12°C. Winter morning lows can occasionally reach -30°C/-20 °C in the <a href="/wiki/Alps" title="Alps">Alps</a>, -14°C/-8 °C in Po valley, -7°C in Florence, -4°C in Rome, -2°C in Naples and 2°C in <a href="/wiki/Palermo" title="Palermo">Palermo</a>. In cities like Rome and Milan, strong <a href="/wiki/Urban_heat_island" title="Urban heat island">heat islands</a> can exist, so that inside the urban area, winters can be milder and summers more sultry. In some winter morning there can be just -3°C in Milan's Dome plaza while -8°/-9° in the metropolitan outskirts. Often, the biggest snowfalls happen in February, sometime in January or March; in the Alps, snow falls more in Autumn and overall Spring over 1500 m, because winter is usually marked by cold and dry weeks; while the Apennines see many more snow falls during winter, but they are warmer and less wet in the other seasons; both the mountain chains can see up to 5-10 m of snow along a year at 2000 m; on the highest pikes of Alps, snow may fall even during mid summer, and small to large glaciers are present.</p>
<p>The absolute record low was near -45 °C in the Alps, and the record low near the sea level was -29.0 °C (recorded on January 12, 1985 at San Pietro Capofiume by Bologna), while in the south cities like Catania, Foggia, Lecce or Alghero have experienced highs of 46 °C in some hot summers.</p>
<p><a name="Government_and_politics" id="Government_and_politics"></a></p>
<h2><span class="editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=Italy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Government and politics">edit</a>]</span> <span class="mw-headline">Government and politics</span></h2>
<dl>
<dd>
<div class="noprint"><i>Main article: <a href="/wiki/Politics_of_Italy" title="Politics of Italy">Politics of Italy</a></i></div>
</dd>
</dl>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:152px;"><a href="/wiki/Image:SignorGiorgioNapolitano.jpg" class="internal" title="Giorgio Napolitano, 11th President of the Italian Republic."><img alt="Giorgio Napolitano, 11th President of the Italian Republic." longdesc="/wiki/Image:SignorGiorgioNapolitano.jpg" class="thumbimage" src="http://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/9b/SignorGiorgioNapolitano.jpg/150px-SignorGiorgioNapolitano.jpg" width="150" height="196" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify" style="float:right"><a href="/wiki/Image:SignorGiorgioNapolitano.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"><img src="/skins-1.5/common/images/magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" alt="" /></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Giorgio_Napolitano" title="Giorgio Napolitano">Giorgio Napolitano</a>, 11th <a href="/wiki/President_of_the_Italian_Republic" title="President of the Italian Republic">President of the Italian Republic</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The 1948 <a href="/wiki/Constitution_of_Italy" title="Constitution of Italy">Constitution of Italy</a> established a <a href="/wiki/Bicameral" title="Bicameral">bicameral</a> <a href="/wiki/Parliament" title="Parliament">parliament</a> (<i><a href="/wiki/Italian_Parliament" title="Italian Parliament">Parlamento</a></i>), consisting of a <a href="/wiki/Italian_Chamber_of_Deputies" title="Italian Chamber of Deputies">Chamber of Deputies</a> (<i>Camera dei Deputati</i>) and a <a href="/wiki/Italian_Senate" title="Italian Senate">Senate</a> (<i>Senato della Repubblica</i>), a separate <a href="/wiki/Judiciary" title="Judiciary">judiciary</a>, and an <a href="/wiki/Executive_branch" title="Executive branch">executive branch</a> composed of a Council of Ministers (<a href="/wiki/Cabinet_%28government%29" title="Cabinet (government)">cabinet</a>) (<i>Consiglio dei ministri</i>), headed by the <a href="/wiki/Prime_Minister_of_Italy" title="Prime Minister of Italy">prime minister</a> (<i>Presidente del consiglio dei ministri</i>).</p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/President_of_the_Italian_Republic" title="President of the Italian Republic">President of the Italian Republic</a> (<i>Presidente della Repubblica</i>) is elected for seven years by the parliament sitting jointly with a small number of regional delegates. The president nominates the prime minister, who proposes the other ministers (formally named by the president). The Council of Ministers must retain the support (<i>fiducia</i>) of both houses.</p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:152px;"><a href="/wiki/Image:22-05-07_Prodi_8.JPG" class="internal" title="Romano Prodi has been the Prime Minister of Italy since May 17, 2006"><img alt="Romano Prodi has been the Prime Minister of Italy since May 17, 2006" longdesc="/wiki/Image:22-05-07_Prodi_8.JPG" class="thumbimage" src="http://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b9/22-05-07_Prodi_8.JPG/150px-22-05-07_Prodi_8.JPG" width="150" height="219" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify" style="float:right"><a href="/wiki/Image:22-05-07_Prodi_8.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"><img src="/skins-1.5/common/images/magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" alt="" /></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Romano_Prodi" title="Romano Prodi">Romano Prodi</a> has been the <a href="/wiki/Prime_Minister" title="Prime Minister">Prime Minister</a> of Italy since May 17, 2006</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The houses of <a href="/wiki/Parliament" title="Parliament">parliament</a> are popularly and directly elected through a complex electoral system (latest amendment in 2005) which combines proportional representation with a majority prize for the largest coalition (Chamber). All <a href="/wiki/Italian_Citizenship" title="Italian Citizenship">Italian citizens</a> older than 18 can vote. However, to vote for the senate, the voter must be at least 25 or older. The electoral system in the <a href="/wiki/Senate_of_Italy" title="Senate of Italy">Senate</a> is based upon <a href="/wiki/Regions_of_Italy" title="Regions of Italy">regional</a> representation. During the elections in 2006, the two competing coalitions were separated by few thousand votes, and in the Chamber the centre-left coalition (<i>L'Unione</i>; <a href="/wiki/English_language" title="English language">English</a>: <i><a href="/wiki/The_Union_%28political_coalition%29" title="The Union (political coalition)">The Union</a></i> ) got 345 Deputies against 277 for the centre-right one (<i>Casa delle Libertà</i>; English: <i><a href="/wiki/House_of_Freedoms" title="House of Freedoms">House of Freedoms</a></i>), while in the Senate <i>l'Ulivo</i> got only two Senators more than absolute majority. The <a href="/wiki/Chamber_of_Deputies_of_Italy" title="Chamber of Deputies of Italy">Chamber of Deputies</a> has 630 <a href="/wiki/Member_of_Parliament" title="Member of Parliament">members</a> and the Senate 315 elected senators; in addition, the Senate includes former presidents and appointed senators for life (no more than five) by the President of the Republic according to special constitutional provisions. As of <a href="/wiki/May_15" title="May 15">15 May</a> <a href="/wiki/2006" title="2006">2006</a>, there are seven <a href="/wiki/Senator_for_life" title="Senator for life">life senators</a> (of which three are former Presidents). Both houses are elected for a maximum of five years, but both may be dissolved by the President before the expiration of their normal term if the Parliament is unable to elect a stable government. In the post war history, this has happened in 1972, 1976, 1979, 1983, 1994 and 1996.</p>
<p>A peculiarity of the <a href="/wiki/Italian_Parliament" title="Italian Parliament">Italian Parliament</a> is the representation given to <a href="/wiki/Italian_diaspora" title="Italian diaspora">Italians permanently living abroad</a> (about 2.7 million people). Among the 630 Deputies and the 315 Senators there are respectively 12 and 6 elected in four distinct foreign constituencies. Those members of Parliament were elected for the first time in April 2006 and they enjoy the same rights as members elected in Italy. Legislative bills may originate in either house and must be passed by a majority in both. The Italian judicial system is based on <a href="/wiki/Roman_law" title="Roman law">Roman law</a> modified by the <a href="/wiki/Napoleonic_code" title="Napoleonic code">Napoleonic code</a> and later statutes. The <a href="/wiki/Constitutional_Court_of_Italy" title="Constitutional Court of Italy">Constitutional Court of Italy</a> (<i>Corte Costituzionale</i>) rules on the conformity of laws with the <a href="/wiki/Constitution_of_Italy" title="Constitution of Italy">Constitution</a> and is a post-World War II innovation.</p>
<dl>
<dd><span class="boilerplate seealso"><i>See also: <a href="/wiki/List_of_Prime_Ministers_of_Italy" title="List of Prime Ministers of Italy">List of Prime Ministers of Italy</a></i></span></dd>
</dl>
<p><a name="Foreign_relations" id="Foreign_relations"></a></p>
<h2><span class="editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=Italy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Foreign relations">edit</a>]</span> <span class="mw-headline">Foreign relations</span></h2>
<dl>
<dd>
<div class="noprint"><i>Main article: <a href="/wiki/Foreign_relations_of_Italy" title="Foreign relations of Italy">Foreign relations of Italy</a></i></div>
</dd>
</dl>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a href="/wiki/Image:D%27Alema_et_Rice.jpg" class="internal" title="Massimo D'Alema, Italian Minister of Foreign Affairs, and U.S. Secretary of State Condoleezza Rice."><img alt="Massimo D'Alema, Italian Minister of Foreign Affairs, and U.S. Secretary of State Condoleezza Rice." longdesc="/wiki/Image:D%27Alema_et_Rice.jpg" class="thumbimage" src="http://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/02/D%27Alema_et_Rice.jpg/200px-D%27Alema_et_Rice.jpg" width="200" height="151" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify" style="float:right"><a href="/wiki/Image:D%27Alema_et_Rice.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"><img src="/skins-1.5/common/images/magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" alt="" /></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Massimo_D%27Alema" title="Massimo D'Alema">Massimo D'Alema</a>, Italian Minister of Foreign Affairs, and U.S. Secretary of State <a href="/wiki/Condoleezza_Rice" title="Condoleezza Rice">Condoleezza Rice</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Italy was a founding member of the European Community--now the <a href="/wiki/European_Union" title="European Union">European Union</a> (EU). Italy was admitted to the <a href="/wiki/United_Nations" title="United Nations">United Nations</a> in 1955 and is a member and strong supporter of the <a href="/wiki/North_Atlantic_Treaty_Organization" title="North Atlantic Treaty Organization">North Atlantic Treaty Organization</a> (NATO), the <a href="/wiki/Organization_for_Economic_Cooperation_and_Development" title="Organization for Economic Cooperation and Development">Organization for Economic Cooperation and Development</a> (OECD), the General Agreement on Tariffs and Trade/World Trade Organization (GATT/WTO), the Organization for Security and Cooperation in Europe (OSCE), and the <a href="/wiki/Council_of_Europe" title="Council of Europe">Council of Europe</a>. It chaired the CSCE (the forerunner of the OSCE) in 1994, the EU in 1996, and the <a href="/wiki/G-8" title="G-8">G-8</a> in 2001 and served as EU president from July to December 2003.</p>
<p>Italy firmly supports the United Nations and its international security activities. Italy actively participated in and deployed troops in support of UN peacekeeping missions in <a href="/wiki/Somalia" title="Somalia">Somalia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mozambique" title="Mozambique">Mozambique</a>, and East Timor and provides critical support for NATO and UN operations in <a href="/wiki/Bosnia_and_Herzegovina" title="Bosnia and Herzegovina">Bosnia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kosovo" title="Kosovo">Kosovo</a> and <a href="/wiki/Albania" title="Albania">Albania</a>. Italy deployed 1,000 <a href="/wiki/Alpini" title="Alpini">Alpini</a> troops to <a href="/wiki/Afghanistan" title="Afghanistan">Afghanistan</a> in support of Operation Enduring Freedom (OEF) in February 2003. Italy also supports international efforts to reconstruct and stabilize <a href="/wiki/Iraq" title="Iraq">Iraq</a> through its military contingent of some 3,200 troops, as well as humanitarian workers and other officials. The troops remained in Iraq under UN mandate and at the request of the sovereign Iraqi Government until December 2006.</p>
<p>In August 2006 Italy sent about 3,000 soldiers to <a href="/wiki/Lebanon" title="Lebanon">Lebanon</a> for the <a href="/wiki/ONU" title="ONU">ONU</a> <a href="/wiki/Peacekeeping" title="Peacekeeping">peacekeeping mission</a> <a href="/wiki/UNIFIL" title="UNIFIL">UNIFIL</a>.<sup id="_ref-NewsMax_0" class="reference"><a href="#_note-NewsMax" title="">[6]</a></sup> Furthermore, since <a href="/wiki/February_2" title="February 2">2 February</a> <a href="/wiki/2007" title="2007">2007</a> an Italian, <a href="/w/index.php?title=Claudio_Graziano&amp;action=edit" class="new" title="Claudio Graziano">Claudio Graziano</a> is the commander of the UN force in the country.</p>
<p>The Italian Government seeks to obtain consensus with other European countries on various defense and security issues within the EU as well as NATO. European integration and the development of common defense and security policies will continue to be of primary interest to Italy.</p>
<dl>
<dd><span class="boilerplate seealso"><i>See also: <a href="/wiki/Italian_Minister_of_Foreign_Affairs" title="Italian Minister of Foreign Affairs">Italian Minister of Foreign Affairs</a></i></span></dd>
</dl>
<p><a name="Military" id="Military"></a></p>
<h2><span class="editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=Italy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Military">edit</a>]</span> <span class="mw-headline">Military</span></h2>
<dl>
<dd>
<div class="noprint"><i>Main article: <a href="/wiki/Military_of_Italy" title="Military of Italy">Military of Italy</a></i></div>
</dd>
</dl>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a href="/wiki/Image:Ariete.jpg" class="internal" title="An Iveco-Oto Melara Ariete main battle tank of Esercito Italiano"><img alt="An Iveco-Oto Melara Ariete main battle tank of Esercito Italiano" longdesc="/wiki/Image:Ariete.jpg" class="thumbimage" src="http://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/07/Ariete.jpg/200px-Ariete.jpg" width="200" height="132" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify" style="float:right"><a href="/wiki/Image:Ariete.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"><img src="/skins-1.5/common/images/magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" alt="" /></a></div>
An <a href="/wiki/Iveco" title="Iveco">Iveco</a>-<a href="/wiki/Oto_Melara" title="Oto Melara">Oto Melara</a> <a href="/wiki/Ariete" title="Ariete">Ariete</a> main battle tank of <a href="/wiki/Esercito_Italiano" title="Esercito Italiano">Esercito Italiano</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The Italian <a href="/wiki/Armed_forces" title="Armed forces">armed forces</a> are divided into four branches:</p>
<ul>
<li><a href="/wiki/Italian_Army" title="Italian Army">Esercito Italiano</a> (Army)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Aeronautica_Militare" title="Aeronautica Militare">Aeronautica Militare</a> (Air Force)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Marina_Militare" title="Marina Militare">Marina Militare</a> (Navy)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Carabinieri" title="Carabinieri">Carabinieri</a> (Military police)</li>
</ul>
<p>The Italian <a href="/wiki/Armed_forces" title="Armed forces">armed forces</a> are under the command of the Italian Supreme Defense Council, presided over by the <a href="/wiki/President_of_the_Italian_Republic" title="President of the Italian Republic">President of the Italian Republic</a>. The total number of military personnel is approximately 308,000. Italy has the eighth highest <a href="/wiki/Military_expenditure" title="Military expenditure">military expenditure</a> in the world.<sup id="_ref-3" class="reference"><a href="#_note-3" title="">[7]</a></sup></p>
<p>The <b>Italian Army</b> (Esercito Italiano) is the ground defense force of the Italian Republic. It has recently (July 29th, 2004) become a professional all-volunteer force of 115,687 <a href="/wiki/Active_duty" title="Active duty">active duty</a> personnel. Its most famous combat vehicles are <a href="/wiki/Dardo_IFV" title="Dardo IFV">Dardo</a>, <a href="/wiki/Centauro" title="Centauro">Centauro</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ariete" title="Ariete">Ariete</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Agusta_A129_Mangusta" title="Agusta A129 Mangusta">Mangusta</a> attack helicopters, recently deployed in UN missions; but the Esercito Italiano also has at its disposal a large number of <a href="/wiki/Leopard_1" title="Leopard 1">Leopard 1</a> and <a href="/wiki/M113" title="M113">M113</a> armored cars.</p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a href="/wiki/Image:AgustaA129_03.jpg" class="internal" title="Agusta A129 Mangusta"><img alt="Agusta A129 Mangusta" longdesc="/wiki/Image:AgustaA129_03.jpg" class="thumbimage" src="http://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3a/AgustaA129_03.jpg/200px-AgustaA129_03.jpg" width="200" height="133" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify" style="float:right"><a href="/wiki/Image:AgustaA129_03.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"><img src="/skins-1.5/common/images/magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" alt="" /></a></div>
Agusta A129 Mangusta</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The <b>Aeronautica Militare Italiana</b> (AMI) is the <a href="/wiki/Air_force" title="Air force">air force</a> of Italy. It was founded as an independent service arm on the <a href="/wiki/March_28" title="March 28">28th March</a>, <a href="/wiki/1923" title="1923">1923</a>, by <a href="/wiki/Victor_Emmanuel_III_of_Italy" title="Victor Emmanuel III of Italy">King Vittorio Emanuele III</a> as the <i><a href="/wiki/Regia_Aeronautica" title="Regia Aeronautica">Regia Aeronautica</a></i> (which equates to "Royal Air Force"). After <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a>, when Italy was made a <a href="/wiki/Republic" title="Republic">republic</a> by referendum, the <i>Regia Aeronautica</i> was given its current name. Today the Aeronautica Militare has a strength of 45,879 and operates 585 aircraft, including 219 combat jets and 114 helicopters. As a stopgap and as replacement for leased <a href="/wiki/Panavia_Tornado" title="Panavia Tornado">Tornado ADV</a> interceptors, the AMI has leased 30 <a href="/wiki/F-16_Fighting_Falcon" title="F-16 Fighting Falcon">F-16A</a> Block 15 ADF and four F-16B Block 10 Fighting Falcons, with an option for some more. The coming years also will see the introduction of 121 <a href="/wiki/Eurofighter_Typhoon" title="Eurofighter Typhoon">EF2000 Eurofighter Typhoons</a>, replacing the leased F-16 Fighting Falcons. Furthermore updates are foreseen on the Tornado IDS/IDT and the AMX-fleet. The transport capacity will be improved with the delivery of eighteen C-130Js (for 2°Gr) and an upgrade programme for the C-130Hs. Also a completely-new developed G222, called C-27J Spartan, will enter service replacing the G222's.</p>
<p>The <b>Marina Militare</b> (the <i>Italian Navy</i>) is one of the four branches of the <a href="/wiki/Military_of_Italy" title="Military of Italy">military forces</a> of Italy. It was born in 1946, as the Navy of the Italian Republic, from the ashes of the <a href="/wiki/Regia_Marina" title="Regia Marina">Regia Marina</a>. Today's Marina Militare is a modern <a href="/wiki/Navy" title="Navy">navy</a> with a strength of 35,261 and ships of every type, such as <a href="/wiki/Aircraft_carrier" title="Aircraft carrier">aircraft carriers</a>, <a href="/wiki/Destroyer" title="Destroyer">destroyers</a>, modern <a href="/wiki/Frigate" title="Frigate">frigates</a>, submarines, amphibious ships and plenty of other smaller ships, including oceanographic research ships.</p>
<p>The Marina Militare is now equipping herself with a bigger <a href="/wiki/Aircraft_carrier" title="Aircraft carrier">aircraft carrier</a> (the <i><a href="/wiki/Cavour_%28550%29" title="Cavour (550)">Cavour</a></i>), new <a href="/wiki/Destroyer" title="Destroyer">destroyers</a>, submarines and multipurpose <a href="/wiki/Frigate" title="Frigate">frigates</a>. In modern times, the Marina Militare, being a member of the North Atlantic Treaty Organisation (NATO), has taken part in many coalition peacekeeping operations. The Marina Militare is considered the fifth strongest navy of the world <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Marina_Militare" class="external autonumber" title="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Marina_Militare" rel="nofollow">[8]</a>.</p>
<p>The <b>Carabinieri</b> are the <a href="/wiki/Gendarmerie" title="Gendarmerie">gendarmerie</a> and <a href="/wiki/Military_police" title="Military police">military police</a> of Italy. At the <a href="/wiki/Sea_Island%2C_Georgia" title="Sea Island, Georgia">Sea Islands Conference</a> of the <a href="/wiki/G8" title="G8">G8</a> in 2004, the Carabinieri was given the mandate to establish a Center of Excellence for Stability Police Units (CoESPU) to spearhead the development of training and doctrinal standards for civilian police units attached to international peacekeeping missions.<sup id="_ref-4" class="reference"><a href="#_note-4" title="">[8]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/As_of_2007" title="As of 2007">As of 2007</a> the Carabinieri has a strength of 112,226.<sup class="noprint Template-Fact"><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources since April 2007" style="white-space: nowrap;">[<i>citation needed</i>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Article 11 of the <a href="/wiki/Italian_Constitution" title="Italian Constitution">Italian Constitution</a> says: "Italy rejects war as an instrument of aggression against the freedoms of others peoples and as a means for settling international controversies; it agrees, on conditions of equality with other states, to the limitations of sovereignty necessary for an order that ensures peace and justice among Nations; it promotes and encourages international organizations having such ends in view.".</p>
<p><a name="Administrative_divisions" id="Administrative_divisions"></a></p>
<h2><span class="editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=Italy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Administrative divisions">edit</a>]</span> <span class="mw-headline">Administrative divisions</span></h2>
<dl>
<dd>
<div class="noprint"><i>Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Regions_of_Italy" title="Regions of Italy">Regions of Italy</a>, <a href="/wiki/Provinces_of_Italy" title="Provinces of Italy">Provinces of Italy</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Municipalities_of_Italy" title="Municipalities of Italy">Municipalities of Italy</a></i></div>
</dd>
</dl>
<p>Italy is subdivided into 20 regions (<i>regioni</i>, singular <i>regione</i>). Five of these regions enjoy a <a href="/wiki/Autonomous_regions_with_special_statute" title="Autonomous regions with special statute">special autonomous status</a> that enables them to enact legislation on some of their specific local matters, and are marked by an *. It is further divided into 109 provinces and 8,101 municipalities (<i>comuni</i>).</p>
